<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Coal</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Coal_(disambiguation)" title="Coal (disambiguation)">Coal (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<table class="infobox" style="width:22em"><caption>Coal</caption><tbody><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;background-color: #cddeff">
<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sedimentary" title="Sedimentary">Sedimentary rock</a></td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">
<a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Coal_anthracite.jpg"><img alt="Coal anthracite.jpg" data-file-height="337" data-file-width="360" height="242" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Coal_anthracite.jpg/258px-Coal_anthracite.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/72/Coal_anthracite.jpg 1.5x" width="258"/></a><div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anthracite_coal" title="Anthracite coal">Anthracite coal</a></div></td></tr><tr><th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;background-color: #ccc">Composition</th></tr><tr><th scope="row">Primary</th><td>

</div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Coal_bituminous.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="308" data-file-width="360" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Coal_bituminous.jpg/220px-Coal_bituminous.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Coal_bituminous.jpg/330px-Coal_bituminous.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/20/Coal_bituminous.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Coal_bituminous.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Bituminous_coal" title="Bituminous coal">Bituminous coal</a></div></div></div>
<p><span id="coal_seam"></span><b>Coal</b> is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Combustible" title="Combustible">combustible</a> black or brownish-black <a href="/wiki/Sedimentary_rock" title="Sedimentary rock">sedimentary rock</a> usually occurring in <a href="/wiki/Stratum" title="Stratum">rock strata</a> in layers or <a href="/wiki/Vein_(geology)" title="Vein (geology)">veins</a> called <b>coal beds</b> or <b>coal seams</b>. The harder forms, such as <a href="/wiki/Anthracite" title="Anthracite">anthracite coal</a>, can be regarded as <a href="/wiki/Metamorphic_rock" title="Metamorphic rock">metamorphic rock</a> because of later exposure to elevated temperature and <a href="/wiki/Pressure" title="Pressure">pressure</a>. Coal is composed primarily of <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a>, along with variable quantities of other elements, chiefly <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> Coal is a <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">fossil fuel</a> that forms when dead <a href="/wiki/Plant_matter" title="Plant matter">plant matter</a> is converted into <a href="/wiki/Peat" title="Peat">peat</a>, which in turn is converted into <a href="/wiki/Lignite" title="Lignite">lignite</a>, then sub-bituminous coal, after that <a href="/wiki/Bituminous_coal" title="Bituminous coal">bituminous coal</a>, and lastly <a href="/wiki/Anthracite" title="Anthracite">anthracite</a>. This involves biological and geological processes. The geological processes take place over millions of years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EIACoalExplained_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-EIACoalExplained-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p><p>Throughout human history, coal has been used as an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Energy_resource" title="Energy resource">energy resource</a>, primarily burned for the production of electricity and heat, and is also used for industrial purposes, such as refining metals. Coal is the largest source of energy for the <a href="/wiki/Electricity_generation" title="Electricity generation">generation of electricity</a> worldwide, as well as one of the largest worldwide <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/anthropogenic" title="wiktionary:anthropogenic">anthropogenic</a> sources of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> releases. The extraction of coal, its use in energy production and its byproducts are all associated with <a href="/wiki/Environmental_impact_of_the_coal_industry" title="Environmental impact of the coal industry">environmental</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coal_worker%27s_pneumoconiosis" title="Coal worker's pneumoconiosis">health effects</a> including <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-psr_coal-damages-health_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-psr_coal-damages-health-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>Coal is extracted from the ground by <a href="/wiki/Coal_mining" title="Coal mining">coal mining</a>. Since 1983, the world's top coal producer has been <a href="/wiki/Coal_in_China" title="Coal in China">China</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BPReview2016_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-BPReview2016-4">[4]</a></sup> In 2015 China produced 3.747 billion tonnes of coal – 48% of 7.861 billion tonnes world coal production. In 2015 other large producers were <a href="/wiki/Coal_mining_in_the_United_States" title="Coal mining in the United States">United States</a> (813 million tonnes), <a href="/wiki/Coal_mining_in_India" title="Coal mining in India">India</a> (678), <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> (539) and <a href="/wiki/Coal_in_Australia" title="Coal in Australia">Australia</a> (503).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BPReview2016_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-BPReview2016-4">[4]</a></sup> In 2010 the largest exporters were Australia with 328 million tonnes (27% of world coal export) and Indonesia with 316 million tonnes (26%),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ei_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-ei-5">[5]</a></sup> while the largest importers were Japan with 207 million tonnes (18% of world coal import), China with 195 million tonnes (17%) and South Korea with 126 million tonnes (11%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eim_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-eim-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h2>
<p>The word originally took the form <i>col</i> in <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a>, from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proto-Germanic" title="Proto-Germanic">Proto-Germanic</a> *<i>kula</i>(<i>n</i>), which in turn is hypothesized to come from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-European" title="Proto-Indo-European">Proto-Indo-European</a> root *<i>g</i>(<i>e</i>)<i>u-lo-</i> "live coal".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-etym_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-etym-7">[7]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Germanic_languages" title="Germanic languages">Germanic</a> cognates include the <a href="/wiki/Old_Frisian" title="Old Frisian">Old Frisian</a> <i>kole</i>, <a href="/wiki/Middle_Dutch" title="Middle Dutch">Middle Dutch</a> <i>cole</i>, <a href="/wiki/Dutch_language" title="Dutch language">Dutch</a> <i>kool</i>, <a href="/wiki/Old_High_German" title="Old High German">Old High German</a> <i>chol</i>, <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a> <i>Kohle</i> and <a href="/wiki/Old_Norse" title="Old Norse">Old Norse</a> <i>kol</i>, and the <a href="/wiki/Irish_language" title="Irish language">Irish</a> word <i>gual</i> is also a cognate via the <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">Indo-European</a> root.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-etym_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-etym-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Formation">Formation</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Struktura_chemiczna_w%C4%99gla_kamiennego.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="900" data-file-width="1100" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Struktura_chemiczna_w%C4%99gla_kamiennego.svg/220px-Struktura_chemiczna_w%C4%99gla_kamiennego.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Struktura_chemiczna_w%C4%99gla_kamiennego.svg/330px-Struktura_chemiczna_w%C4%99gla_kamiennego.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Struktura_chemiczna_w%C4%99gla_kamiennego.svg/440px-Struktura_chemiczna_w%C4%99gla_kamiennego.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Struktura_chemiczna_w%C4%99gla_kamiennego.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Example chemical structure of coal</div></div></div>
<p>At various times in the geologic past, the Earth had dense forests<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> in low-lying wetland areas. Due to natural processes such as flooding, these forests were buried underneath soil. As more and more soil deposited over them, they were compressed. The temperature also rose as they sank deeper and deeper. As the process continued the plant matter was protected from <a href="/wiki/Biodegradation" title="Biodegradation">biodegradation</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxidation" title="Oxidation">oxidation</a>, usually by mud or acidic water. This trapped the carbon in immense <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Peat_bog" title="Peat bog">peat bogs</a> that were eventually covered and deeply buried by sediments. Under high pressure and high temperature, dead vegetation was slowly converted to coal. As coal contains mainly carbon, the conversion of dead vegetation into coal is called carbonization.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p><p>The wide, shallow seas of the <a href="/wiki/Carboniferous" title="Carboniferous">Carboniferous</a> Period provided ideal conditions for coal formation, although coal is known from most geological periods. The exception is the coal gap in the <a href="/wiki/Permian%E2%80%93Triassic_extinction_event" title="Permian–Triassic extinction event">Permian–Triassic extinction event</a>, where coal is rare. Coal is known from <a href="/wiki/Precambrian" title="Precambrian">Precambrian</a> strata, which predate land plants—this coal is presumed to have originated from residues of algae.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tyler1957_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tyler1957-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mancuso1981_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mancuso1981-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ranks">Ranks</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sydney_Mines_Point_Aconi_Seam_038.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2016" data-file-width="3024" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Sydney_Mines_Point_Aconi_Seam_038.JPG/220px-Sydney_Mines_Point_Aconi_Seam_038.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Sydney_Mines_Point_Aconi_Seam_038.JPG/330px-Sydney_Mines_Point_Aconi_Seam_038.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Sydney_Mines_Point_Aconi_Seam_038.JPG/440px-Sydney_Mines_Point_Aconi_Seam_038.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sydney_Mines_Point_Aconi_Seam_038.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Coastal exposure of the Point Aconi Seam (Nova Scotia)</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Coal_Rank_USGS.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2665" data-file-width="2132" height="275" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Coal_Rank_USGS.png/220px-Coal_Rank_USGS.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Coal_Rank_USGS.png/330px-Coal_Rank_USGS.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Coal_Rank_USGS.png/440px-Coal_Rank_USGS.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Coal_Rank_USGS.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Coal ranking system used in the United States (US Geological Survey)</div></div></div>
<p>As geological processes apply <a href="/wiki/Pressure" title="Pressure">pressure</a> to dead <a href="/wiki/Biotic_material" title="Biotic material">biotic material</a> over time, under suitable conditions, its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metamorphic_grade" title="Metamorphic grade">metamorphic grade</a> increases successively into:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Peat" title="Peat">Peat</a>, considered to be a precursor of coal, which has industrial importance as a fuel in some regions, for example, Ireland and Finland (In its dehydrated form, peat is a highly effective absorbent for fuel and <a href="/wiki/Oil_spill" title="Oil spill">oil spills</a> on land and water, and also used as a conditioner for soil to make it more able to retain and slowly release water.)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lignite" title="Lignite">Lignite</a>, or brown coal, the lowest rank of coal, used almost exclusively as fuel for electric power generation
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Jet_(lignite)" title="Jet (lignite)">Jet</a>, a compact form of lignite, sometimes polished; used as an ornamental stone since the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Upper_Palaeolithic" title="Upper Palaeolithic">Upper Palaeolithic</a></li></ul></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Sub-bituminous_coal" title="Sub-bituminous coal">Sub-bituminous coal</a>, whose properties range between those of lignite and those of bituminous coal (It is used primarily as fuel for steam-electric power generation and is also an important source of light <a href="/wiki/Aromatic_hydrocarbon" title="Aromatic hydrocarbon">aromatic hydrocarbons</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Chemical_synthesis" title="Chemical synthesis">chemical synthesis</a> industry.)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Bituminous_coal" title="Bituminous coal">Bituminous coal</a>, a dense sedimentary rock, usually black, but sometimes dark brown, often with well-defined bands of bright and dull material (It is used primarily as fuel in steam-electric power generation, with substantial quantities used for heat and power applications in manufacturing and to make <a href="/wiki/Coke_(fuel)" title="Coke (fuel)">coke</a>.)</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Steam_coal" title="Steam coal">Steam coal</a>, a grade between bituminous coal and anthracite (It was once widely used as a fuel for <a href="/wiki/Steam_locomotive" title="Steam locomotive">steam locomotives</a>. In this specialized use, it is sometimes known as "sea coal" in the US.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> Small steam coal, also called <i>dry small steam nuts</i> (or DSSN) was used as a fuel for domestic <a href="/wiki/Water_heating" title="Water heating">water heating</a>.)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Anthracite" title="Anthracite">Anthracite</a>, the highest rank of coal (It is a harder, glossy black coal used primarily for residential and commercial <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Space_heating" title="Space heating">space heating</a>; it may be divided further into metamorphically altered bituminous coal and "petrified oil", as from the deposits in Pennsylvania.)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Graphite" title="Graphite">Graphite</a> (It is one of the more difficult coals to ignite and not commonly used as fuel; it is most used in pencils, or powdered for <a href="/wiki/Lubrication" title="Lubrication">lubrication</a>.)</li></ul>
<p>The classification of coal is generally based on the content of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coal_volatiles" title="Coal volatiles">volatiles</a>. However, the exact classification varies between countries. According to the German classification, coal is classified as follows:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p>

<p>The middle six grades in the table represent a progressive transition from the English-language sub-bituminous to bituminous coal. The last class is an approximate equivalent to anthracite, but more inclusive. (US anthracite has &lt;6% volatiles.)<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Vagueness" title="Wikipedia:Vagueness"><span title="This information is too vague. (September 2017)">vague</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Cannel_coal" title="Cannel coal">Cannel coal</a> (sometimes called "candle coal") is a variety of fine-grained, high-rank coal with significant hydrogen content. It consists primarily of "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exinite" title="Exinite">exinite</a>" <a href="/wiki/Maceral" title="Maceral">macerals</a>, now termed "liptinite".
</p>
<h3><span id="Hilt.27s_law"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Hilt's_law">Hilt's law</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hilt%27s_law" title="Hilt's law">Hilt's law</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Hilt%27s_law" title="Hilt's law">Hilt's law</a> is a geological observation that (within in a small area) the deeper the coal is found, the higher its rank (or grade). It applies if the thermal gradient is entirely vertical; however, <a href="/wiki/Metamorphism" title="Metamorphism">metamorphism</a> may cause lateral changes of rank, irrespective of depth.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_uses_as_fuel">Early uses as fuel</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_coal_mining" title="History of coal mining">History of coal mining</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tiangong_Kaiwu_Coal_Mining.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="330" data-file-width="212" height="265" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/2/27/Tiangong_Kaiwu_Coal_Mining.gif/170px-Tiangong_Kaiwu_Coal_Mining.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/2/27/Tiangong_Kaiwu_Coal_Mining.gif 1.5x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tiangong_Kaiwu_Coal_Mining.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Chinese coal miners in an illustration of the <i>Tiangong Kaiwu</i> encyclopedia, published in 1637</div></div></div>
<p>The earliest recognized use is from the <a href="/wiki/Shenyang" title="Shenyang">Shenyang</a> area of China 4000 BC where <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> inhabitants had begun carving ornaments from black lignite.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> Coal from the Fushun mine in northeastern China was used to smelt copper as early as 1000 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Marco_Polo" title="Marco Polo">Marco Polo</a>, the Italian who traveled to China in the 13th century, described coal as "black stones ... which burn like logs", and said coal was so plentiful, people could take three hot baths a week.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> In Europe, the earliest reference to the use of coal as fuel is from the geological treatise <i>On stones</i> (Lap. 16) by the Greek scientist <a href="/wiki/Theophrastus" title="Theophrastus">Theophrastus</a> (c. 371–287 BC):<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mattusch_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mattusch-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p> Among the materials that are dug because they are useful, those known as <i>anthrakes</i> [coals] are made of earth, and, once set on fire, they burn like charcoal. They are found in Liguria ... and in Elis as one approaches Olympia by the mountain road; and they are used by those who work in metals.</p><div class="templatequotecite">— <cite>Theophrastus, On Stones (16) <i>translation</i></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p><a href="/wiki/Outcrop" title="Outcrop">Outcrop</a> coal was used in Britain during the <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a> (3000–2000 BC), where it has been detected as forming part of the composition of <a href="/wiki/Funeral" title="Funeral">funeral</a> <a href="/wiki/Pyre" title="Pyre">pyres</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannicacoal_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Britannicacoal-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Roman_Britain" title="Roman Britain">Roman Britain</a>, with the exception of two modern fields, "the <a href="/wiki/Mining_in_Roman_Britain" title="Mining in Roman Britain">Romans</a> were exploiting coals in all the major coalfields in <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a> by the end of the second century AD".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith_1997,_322–324_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Smith_1997,_322–324-24">[24]</a></sup> Evidence of trade in coal (dated to about AD 200) has been found at the <a href="/wiki/Heronbridge_Roman_Site" title="Heronbridge Roman Site">Roman settlement at Heronbridge</a>, near <a href="/wiki/Chester" title="Chester">Chester</a>, and in the <a href="/wiki/The_Fens" title="The Fens">Fenlands</a> of <a href="/wiki/East_Anglia" title="East Anglia">East Anglia</a>, where coal from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/English_Midlands" title="English Midlands">Midlands</a> was transported via the <a href="/wiki/Car_Dyke" title="Car Dyke">Car Dyke</a> for use in drying grain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> Coal cinders have been found in the hearths of <a href="/wiki/Roman_villa" title="Roman villa">villas</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_forts" title="Roman forts">Roman forts</a>, particularly in <a href="/wiki/Northumberland" title="Northumberland">Northumberland</a>, dated to around AD 400. In the west of England, contemporary writers described the wonder of a permanent brazier of coal on the altar of <a href="/wiki/Minerva" title="Minerva">Minerva</a> at <a href="/wiki/Aquae_Sulis" title="Aquae Sulis">Aquae Sulis</a> (modern day <a href="/wiki/Bath,_Somerset" title="Bath, Somerset">Bath</a>), although in fact easily accessible surface coal from what became the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Somerset_coalfield" title="Somerset coalfield">Somerset coalfield</a> was in common use in quite lowly dwellings locally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> Evidence of coal's use for iron-working in the city during the Roman period has been found.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Eschweiler" title="Eschweiler">Eschweiler</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rhineland" title="Rhineland">Rhineland</a>, deposits of <a href="/wiki/Bituminous_coal" title="Bituminous coal">bituminous coal</a> were used by the Romans for the smelting of <a href="/wiki/Iron_ore" title="Iron ore">iron ore</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith_1997,_322–324_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-Smith_1997,_322–324-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Men_of_the_Mine-_Life_at_the_Coal_Face,_Britain,_1942_D8263.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="533" data-file-width="800" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Men_of_the_Mine-_Life_at_the_Coal_Face%2C_Britain%2C_1942_D8263.jpg/220px-Men_of_the_Mine-_Life_at_the_Coal_Face%2C_Britain%2C_1942_D8263.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Men_of_the_Mine-_Life_at_the_Coal_Face%2C_Britain%2C_1942_D8263.jpg/330px-Men_of_the_Mine-_Life_at_the_Coal_Face%2C_Britain%2C_1942_D8263.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Men_of_the_Mine-_Life_at_the_Coal_Face%2C_Britain%2C_1942_D8263.jpg/440px-Men_of_the_Mine-_Life_at_the_Coal_Face%2C_Britain%2C_1942_D8263.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Men_of_the_Mine-_Life_at_the_Coal_Face,_Britain,_1942_D8263.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Coal miner in Britain, 1942</div></div></div>
<p>No evidence exists of the product being of great importance in Britain before the <a href="/wiki/High_Middle_Ages" title="High Middle Ages">High Middle Ages</a>, after about AD 1000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TCC_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-TCC-28">[28]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mineral" title="Mineral">Mineral</a> coal came to be referred to as "seacoal" in the 13th century; the wharf where the material arrived in London was known as Seacoal Lane, so identified in a charter of <a href="/wiki/Henry_III_of_England" title="Henry III of England">King Henry III</a> granted in 1253.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> Initially, the name was given because much coal was found on the shore, having fallen from the exposed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coal_seam" title="Coal seam">coal seams</a> on cliffs above or washed out of underwater coal outcrops,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TCC_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-TCC-28">[28]</a></sup> but by the time of <a href="/wiki/Henry_VIII_of_England" title="Henry VIII of England">Henry VIII</a>, it was understood to derive from the way it was carried to London by sea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> In 1257–1259, coal from <a href="/wiki/Newcastle_upon_Tyne" title="Newcastle upon Tyne">Newcastle upon Tyne</a> was shipped to London for the smiths and lime-burners building <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Abbey" title="Westminster Abbey">Westminster Abbey</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TCC_28-2"><a href="#cite_note-TCC-28">[28]</a></sup> Seacoal Lane and Newcastle Lane, where coal was unloaded at wharves along the <a href="/wiki/River_Fleet" title="River Fleet">River Fleet</a>, are still in existence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> (See <a href="/wiki/Coal#Industrial_processes" title="Coal">Industrial processes</a> below for modern uses of the term.)
</p><p>These easily accessible sources had largely become exhausted (or could not meet the growing demand) by the 13th century, when underground extraction by <a href="/wiki/Shaft_mining" title="Shaft mining">shaft mining</a> or <a href="/wiki/Adit" title="Adit">adits</a> was developed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannicacoal_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-Britannicacoal-22">[22]</a></sup> The alternative name was "pitcoal", because it came from mines. The development of the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a> led to the large-scale use of coal, as the <a href="/wiki/Steam_engine" title="Steam engine">steam engine</a> took over from the <a href="/wiki/Water_wheel" title="Water wheel">water wheel</a>. In 1700, five-sixths of the world's coal was mined in Britain. Britain would have run out of suitable sites for watermills by the 1830s if coal had not been available as a source of energy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wrigley_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wrigley-32">[32]</a></sup> In 1947, there were some 750,000 miners in Britain,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC_News_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_News-33">[33]</a></sup> but by 2004, this had shrunk to some 5,000 miners working in around 20 collieries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC_Inside_Out_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_Inside_Out-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Uses_today">Uses today</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Castle_Gate_Power_Plant,_Utah_2007.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1439" data-file-width="2293" height="138" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Castle_Gate_Power_Plant%2C_Utah_2007.jpg/220px-Castle_Gate_Power_Plant%2C_Utah_2007.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Castle_Gate_Power_Plant%2C_Utah_2007.jpg/330px-Castle_Gate_Power_Plant%2C_Utah_2007.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Castle_Gate_Power_Plant%2C_Utah_2007.jpg/440px-Castle_Gate_Power_Plant%2C_Utah_2007.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Castle_Gate_Power_Plant,_Utah_2007.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Castle_Gate_Power_Plant" title="Castle Gate Power Plant">Castle Gate Power Plant</a> near <a href="/wiki/Helper,_Utah" title="Helper, Utah">Helper, Utah</a>, US</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ashtabulacoalcars_e2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="634" data-file-width="897" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Ashtabulacoalcars_e2.jpg/220px-Ashtabulacoalcars_e2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Ashtabulacoalcars_e2.jpg/330px-Ashtabulacoalcars_e2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Ashtabulacoalcars_e2.jpg/440px-Ashtabulacoalcars_e2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ashtabulacoalcars_e2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Coal rail cars</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Komatsu_bulldozer_pushing_coal_in_Power_plant_Ljubljana_(winter_2017).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2352" data-file-width="4296" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Komatsu_bulldozer_pushing_coal_in_Power_plant_Ljubljana_%28winter_2017%29.jpg/220px-Komatsu_bulldozer_pushing_coal_in_Power_plant_Ljubljana_%28winter_2017%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Komatsu_bulldozer_pushing_coal_in_Power_plant_Ljubljana_%28winter_2017%29.jpg/330px-Komatsu_bulldozer_pushing_coal_in_Power_plant_Ljubljana_%28winter_2017%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Komatsu_bulldozer_pushing_coal_in_Power_plant_Ljubljana_%28winter_2017%29.jpg/440px-Komatsu_bulldozer_pushing_coal_in_Power_plant_Ljubljana_%28winter_2017%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Komatsu_bulldozer_pushing_coal_in_Power_plant_Ljubljana_(winter_2017).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Komatsu_Limited" title="Komatsu Limited">Komatsu bulldozer</a> pushing coal in <a href="/wiki/Ljubljana_Power_Station" title="Ljubljana Power Station">Ljubljana Power Station</a></div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coal_as_fuel">Coal as fuel</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Electricity_generation" title="Electricity generation">Electricity generation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Clean_coal_technology" title="Clean coal technology">Clean coal technology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">Global warming</a>, and <a href="/wiki/List_of_coal_power_stations" title="List of coal power stations">List of coal power stations</a></div>
<p>Coal is primarily used as a <a href="/wiki/Solid_fuel" title="Solid fuel">solid fuel</a> to produce electricity and heat through combustion. According to the <a href="/wiki/Energy_Information_Administration" title="Energy Information Administration">EIA</a>, world coal consumption is projected to increase from 2012 to 2040 at an average rate of 0.6%/year, from 153 quadrillion Btu (<a href="/wiki/Quad_(unit)" title="Quad (unit)">1 Quad</a> are 36,000,000 tonnes of coal) in 2012 to 169 quadrillion Btu in 2020, and to 180 quadrillion Btu in 2040.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> Efforts around the world to reduce the use of coal has led some regions to switch to natural gas.
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coal_power_in_China" title="Coal power in China">China</a> produced 3.47 billion tonnes (3.83 billion short tons) in 2011. <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> produced about 578 million tonnes (637.1 million short tons) in 2011. 69% of China's electricity comes from coal. The US consumed about 13% of the world total in 2010, i.e. 951 million tonnes (1.05 billion short tons), using 93% of it for generation of electricity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> 46% of total power generated in the US was using coal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> The United States <a href="/wiki/Energy_Information_Administration" title="Energy Information Administration">Energy Information Administration</a> estimates coal <a href="/wiki/Mineral_resource_classification" title="Mineral resource classification">reserves</a> at <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7011948000000000000♠</span>948<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>9</sup></span> <a href="/wiki/Short_ton" title="Short ton">short tons</a> (860 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gigatonne" title="Gigatonne">Gt</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EIAreserves_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-EIAreserves-38">[38]</a></sup> One estimate for <a href="/wiki/Mineral_resource_classification" title="Mineral resource classification">resources</a> is 18,000 Gt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>When coal is used for <a href="/wiki/Electricity_generation" title="Electricity generation">electricity generation</a>, it is usually pulverized and then burned in a <a href="/wiki/Furnace" title="Furnace">furnace</a> with a <a href="/wiki/Boiler" title="Boiler">boiler</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-World_Coal_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-World_Coal-40">[40]</a></sup> The furnace heat converts boiler water to <a href="/wiki/Steam" title="Steam">steam</a>, which is then used to spin <a href="/wiki/Turbine" title="Turbine">turbines</a> which turn <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_generator" title="Electrical generator">generators</a> and create electricity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thermodynamic_efficiency" title="Thermodynamic efficiency">thermodynamic efficiency</a> of this process has been improved over time; some older coal-fired power stations have thermal efficiencies in the vicinity of 25%<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> whereas the newest <a href="/wiki/Supercritical_steam_generator" title="Supercritical steam generator">supercritical</a> and "ultra-supercritical" steam cycle turbines, operating at temperatures over 600 °C and pressures over 27 MPa (over 3900 psi), can achieve thermal efficiencies in excess of 45% (LHV basis) using anthracite fuel,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> or around 43% (LHV basis) even when using lower-grade lignite fuel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> Further thermal efficiency improvements are also achievable by improved pre-drying (especially relevant with high-moisture fuel such as lignite or biomass) and cooling technologies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p>An alternative approach of using coal for electricity generation with improved efficiency is the <a href="/wiki/Integrated_gasification_combined_cycle" title="Integrated gasification combined cycle">integrated gasification combined cycle</a> (IGCC) power plant. Instead of pulverizing the coal and burning it directly as fuel in the steam-generating boiler, the coal is gasified (see <a href="/wiki/Coal_gasification" title="Coal gasification">coal gasification</a>) to create <a href="/wiki/Syngas" title="Syngas">syngas</a>, which is burned in a <a href="/wiki/Gas_turbine" title="Gas turbine">gas turbine</a> to produce electricity (just like natural gas is burned in a turbine). Hot exhaust gases from the turbine are used to raise steam in a <a href="/wiki/Heat_recovery_steam_generator" title="Heat recovery steam generator">heat recovery steam generator</a> which powers a supplemental <a href="/wiki/Steam_turbine" title="Steam turbine">steam turbine</a>. Thermal efficiencies of current IGCC power plants range from 39% to 42%<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> (HHV basis) or ≈42–45% (LHV basis) for bituminous coal and assuming utilization of mainstream gasification technologies (Shell, GE Gasifier, CB&amp;I). IGCC power plants outperform conventional pulverized coal-fueled plants in terms of pollutant emissions, and allow for relatively easy carbon capture.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>At least 40% of the world's electricity comes from coal,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-World_Coal_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-World_Coal-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> and in 2016, 30% of the United States' electricity came from coal, down from approximately 49% in 2008.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTKentucky_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYTKentucky-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> As of 2012 in the United States, use of coal to generate electricity was declining, as plentiful supplies of natural gas obtained by <a href="/wiki/Hydraulic_fracturing" title="Hydraulic fracturing">hydraulic fracturing</a> of tight shale formations became available at low prices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTKentucky_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-NYTKentucky-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p>In Denmark, a net electric efficiency of &gt;47% has been obtained at the coal-fired Nordjyllandsværket CHP Plant and an overall plant efficiency of up to 91% with cogeneration of electricity and district heating.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> The multifuel-fired Avedøreværket CHP Plant just outside Copenhagen can achieve a net electric efficiency as high as 49%. The overall plant efficiency with cogeneration of electricity and district heating can reach as much as 94%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p><p>An alternative form of coal combustion is as <a href="/wiki/Coal-water_slurry_fuel" title="Coal-water slurry fuel">coal-water slurry fuel</a> (CWS), which was developed in the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>. Other ways to use coal are <a href="/wiki/Cogeneration" title="Cogeneration">combined heat and power cogeneration</a> and <a href="/wiki/Magnetohydrodynamic_generator" title="Magnetohydrodynamic generator">an MHD topping cycle</a>.
</p><p>The total known deposits recoverable by current technologies, including highly polluting, low-energy content types of coal (i.e., lignite, bituminous), is sufficient for many years.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="This term requires quantification. (May 2012)">quantify</span></a></i>]</sup> Consumption is increasing and <a href="/wiki/Peak_coal" title="Peak coal">maximal production could be reached</a> within decades (see <a href="/wiki/Coal#World_coal_reserves" title="Coal">world coal reserves</a>, below). On the other hand, much may have to be left in the ground to avoid climate change.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Switch_to_natural_gas">Switch to natural gas</span></h4>
<p>Worldwide <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas#Power_generation" title="Natural gas">natural gas generated power</a> has increased from 740 TW in 1973 to 5140 TW in 2014, generating 22% of the worlds total electricity, approximately half as much as generated with coal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> In addition to generating electricity, natural gas is also popular in some countries for heating and as an <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas_vehicle" title="Natural gas vehicle">automotive fuel</a>.
</p><p>The use of <a href="/wiki/Energy_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Energy in the United Kingdom">coal in the United Kingdom</a> declined as a result of the development of <a href="/wiki/North_Sea_oil" title="North Sea oil">North Sea oil</a> and the subsequent <a href="/wiki/Dash_for_Gas" title="Dash for Gas">Dash for Gas</a> from the 1990s to 2000.
</p><p>In Canada some <a href="/wiki/Thermal_power_station" title="Thermal power station">coal power plants</a> such as the <a href="/wiki/Hearn_Generating_Station" title="Hearn Generating Station">Hearn Generating Station</a> have stopped burning coal by switching the plant to natural gas.
</p><p>In the United States, 27 gigawatts of capacity from coal-fired generators was slated to be retired from 175 US coal-fired power plants between 2012 and 2016.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> Natural gas showed a corresponding jump, increasing by a third over 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-epm_312_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-epm_312-58">[58]</a></sup> Coal's share of US electricity generation dropped to just over 36%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-epm_312_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-epm_312-58">[58]</a></sup> Due to <a href="/wiki/Shale_gas_in_the_United_States#Economic_impacts" title="Shale gas in the United States">emergence of shale gas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coal_power_in_the_United_States" title="Coal power in the United States">coal consumption</a> declined from 2009.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> Natural gas accounted for 81% of new power generation in the US between 2000 and 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup>  Coal-fired generation puts out about twice the amount of carbon dioxide—around 2,000 pounds for every megawatt hour generated—than electricity generated by burning natural gas at 1,100 pounds of <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gas</a> per megawatt hour. As the fuel mix in the United States has changed to reduce coal and increase natural gas generation, carbon dioxide emissions have unexpectedly fallen. Those measured in the first quarter of 2012 were the lowest of any recorded for the first quarter of any year since 1992.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coking_coal_and_use_of_coke">Coking coal and use of coke</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Coke_(fuel)" title="Coke (fuel)">Coke (fuel)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Coke_Ovens_Abercwmboi.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2136" data-file-width="3039" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Coke_Ovens_Abercwmboi.jpg/220px-Coke_Ovens_Abercwmboi.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Coke_Ovens_Abercwmboi.jpg/330px-Coke_Ovens_Abercwmboi.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Coke_Ovens_Abercwmboi.jpg/440px-Coke_Ovens_Abercwmboi.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Coke_Ovens_Abercwmboi.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Coke oven at a <a href="/wiki/Smokeless_fuel" title="Smokeless fuel">smokeless fuel</a> plant in <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a>, United Kingdom</div></div></div>
<p>Coke is a solid carbonaceous residue derived from coking coal (a low-ash, low-sulfur bituminous coal, also known as <a href="/wiki/Metallurgical_coal" title="Metallurgical coal">metallurgical coal</a>), which is used in manufacturing <a href="/wiki/Steel" title="Steel">steel</a> and other iron products.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-World_Coal_Steel_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-World_Coal_Steel-62">[62]</a></sup> Coke is made from coking coal by baking in an oven without oxygen at temperatures as high as 1,000 °C (1,832 °F), driving off the volatile constituents and fusing together the fixed carbon and residual ash. Metallurgical coke is used as a fuel and as a <a href="/wiki/Reducing_agent" title="Reducing agent">reducing agent</a> in smelting <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a> ore in a <a href="/wiki/Blast_furnace" title="Blast furnace">blast furnace</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> The result is <a href="/wiki/Pig_iron" title="Pig iron">pig iron</a>, and is too rich in dissolved carbon, so it must be treated further to make steel.
</p><p>Coking coal should be low in ash, <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus" title="Phosphorus">phosphorus</a>, so that these do not migrate to the metal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-World_Coal_Steel_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-World_Coal_Steel-62">[62]</a></sup> Based on the ash percentage, the coking coal can be divided into various grades. These grades are:
</p>
<ul><li>Steel Grade I (Ash content not exceeding 15%)</li>
<li>Steel Grade II (Exceeding 15% but not exceeding 18%)</li>
<li>Washery Grade I (Exceeding 18% but not exceeding 21%)</li>
<li>Washery Grade II (Exceeding 21% but not exceeding 24%)</li>
<li>Washery Grade III (Exceeding 24% but not exceeding 28%)</li>
<li>Washery Grade IV (Exceeding 28% but not exceeding 35%)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>The coke must be <a href="/wiki/Coke_strength_after_reaction" title="Coke strength after reaction">strong enough</a> to resist the weight of overburden in the blast furnace, which is why coking coal is so important in making steel using the conventional route. However, the alternative route is <a href="/wiki/Direct_reduced_iron" title="Direct reduced iron">direct reduced iron</a>, where any carbonaceous fuel can be used to make sponge or pelletised iron. Coke from coal is grey, hard, and porous and has a heating value of 24.8 million Btu/ton (29.6 MJ/kg). Some cokemaking processes produce valuable byproducts, including <a href="/wiki/Coal_tar" title="Coal tar">coal tar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a>, light oils, and <a href="/wiki/Coal_gas" title="Coal gas">coal gas</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Petroleum_coke" title="Petroleum coke">Petroleum coke</a> is the solid residue obtained in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oil_refining" title="Oil refining">oil refining</a>, which resembles coke, but contains too many impurities to be useful in metallurgical applications.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gasification">Gasification</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Coal_gasification" title="Coal gasification">Coal gasification</a> and <a href="/wiki/Underground_coal_gasification" title="Underground coal gasification">Underground coal gasification</a></div>
<p>Coal gasification can be used to produce <a href="/wiki/Syngas" title="Syngas">syngas</a>, a mixture of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a> (CO) and hydrogen (H<sub>2</sub>) gas. Often syngas is used to fire gas turbines to produce electricity, but the versatility of syngas also allows it to be converted into transportation fuels, such as gasoline and diesel, through the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fischer-Tropsch_process" title="Fischer-Tropsch process">Fischer-Tropsch process</a>; alternatively, syngas can be converted into <a href="/wiki/Methanol" title="Methanol">methanol</a>, which can be blended into fuel directly or converted to gasoline via the methanol to gasoline process.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> Gasification combined with Fischer-Tropsch technology is currently used by the <a href="/wiki/Sasol" title="Sasol">Sasol</a> chemical company of <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a> to make motor vehicle fuels from coal and natural gas. Alternatively, the hydrogen obtained from gasification can be used for various purposes, such as powering a <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_economy" title="Hydrogen economy">hydrogen economy</a>, making ammonia, or upgrading fossil fuels.
</p><p>During gasification, the coal is mixed with <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Steam" title="Steam">steam</a> while also being heated and pressurized. During the reaction, oxygen and water molecules <a href="/wiki/Redox" title="Redox">oxidize</a> the coal into carbon monoxide (CO), while also releasing <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> gas (H<sub>2</sub>). This process has been conducted in both underground coal mines and in the production of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Town_gas" title="Town gas">town gas</a> which was piped to customers to burn for illumination, heating, and cooking.
</p>
<dl><dd>3C (<i>as Coal</i>) + O<sub>2</sub> + H<sub>2</sub>O → H<sub>2</sub> + 3CO</dd></dl>
<p>If the refiner wants to produce gasoline, the syngas is collected at this state and routed into a Fischer-Tropsch reaction. If hydrogen is the desired end-product, however, the syngas is fed into the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Water_gas_shift_reaction" title="Water gas shift reaction">water gas shift reaction</a>, where more hydrogen is liberated.
</p>
<dl><dd>CO + H<sub>2</sub>O → CO<sub>2</sub> + H<sub>2</sub></dd></dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Liquefaction">Liquefaction</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Coal_liquefaction" title="Coal liquefaction">Coal liquefaction</a></div>
<p>Coal can also be converted into <a href="/wiki/Synthetic_fuel" title="Synthetic fuel">synthetic fuels</a> equivalent to <a href="/wiki/Gasoline" title="Gasoline">gasoline</a> or <a href="/wiki/Diesel_fuel" title="Diesel fuel">diesel</a> by several different direct processes (which do not intrinsically require gasification or indirect conversion).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> In the direct liquefaction processes, the coal is either <a href="/wiki/Hydrogenation" title="Hydrogenation">hydrogenated</a> or <a href="/wiki/Carbonization" title="Carbonization">carbonized</a>. Hydrogenation processes are the <a href="/wiki/Bergius_process" title="Bergius process">Bergius process</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> the SRC-I and SRC-II (Solvent Refined Coal) processes, the NUS Corporation hydrogenation process<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-handbook2_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-handbook2-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> and several other single-stage and two-stage processes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> In the process of low-temperature <a href="/wiki/Carbonization" title="Carbonization">carbonization</a>, coal is coked at temperatures between 360 and 750 °C (680 and 1,380 °F). These temperatures optimize the production of coal tars richer in lighter hydrocarbons than normal coal tar. The coal tar is then further processed into fuels. An overview of coal liquefaction and its future potential is available.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p><p>Coal liquefaction methods involve carbon dioxide (CO<sub>2</sub>) emissions in the conversion process. If coal liquefaction is done without employing either <a href="/wiki/Carbon_capture_and_storage" title="Carbon capture and storage">carbon capture and storage</a> (CCS) technologies or biomass blending, the result is lifecycle greenhouse gas footprints that are generally greater than those released in the extraction and refinement of liquid fuel production from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crude_oil" title="Crude oil">crude oil</a>. If CCS technologies are employed, reductions of 5–12% can be achieved in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coal_to_liquids" title="Coal to liquids">Coal to Liquid (CTL)</a> plants and up to a 75% reduction is achievable when co-gasifying coal with commercially demonstrated levels of biomass (30% biomass by weight) in coal/biomass-to-liquids plants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> For future <a href="/wiki/Synthetic_fuel" title="Synthetic fuel">synthetic fuel</a> projects, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_sequestration" title="Carbon dioxide sequestration">carbon dioxide sequestration</a> is proposed to avoid releasing CO<sub>2</sub> into the atmosphere. Sequestration adds to the cost of production.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Refined_coal">Refined coal</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Refined_coal" title="Refined coal">Refined coal</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:392px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:5.-Carb%C3%B3_(26655108555).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1428" data-file-width="2998" height="186" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/5.-Carb%C3%B3_%2826655108555%29.jpg/390px-5.-Carb%C3%B3_%2826655108555%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/5.-Carb%C3%B3_%2826655108555%29.jpg/585px-5.-Carb%C3%B3_%2826655108555%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/5.-Carb%C3%B3_%2826655108555%29.jpg/780px-5.-Carb%C3%B3_%2826655108555%29.jpg 2x" width="390"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:5.-Carb%C3%B3_(26655108555).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Traditional works of coal. <a href="/wiki/Valencian_Museum_of_Ethnology" title="Valencian Museum of Ethnology">Valencian Museum of Ethnology</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Refined coal is the product of a coal-upgrading technology that removes moisture and certain pollutants from lower-rank coals such as sub-bituminous and lignite (brown) coals. It is one form of several precombustion treatments and processes for coal that alter coal's characteristics before it is burned. The goals of precombustion coal technologies are to increase efficiency and reduce emissions when the coal is burned. Depending on the situation, precombustion technology can be used in place of or as a supplement to postcombustion technologies to control emissions from coal-fueled boilers.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Industrial_processes">Industrial processes <span id="Industrial_processes"></span><span id="Sea_coal"></span></span></h3>
<p>Finely ground bituminous coal, known in this application as sea coal, is a constituent of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Foundry_sand" title="Foundry sand">foundry sand</a>. While the molten metal is in the <a href="/wiki/Molding_(process)" title="Molding (process)">mould</a>, the coal burns slowly, releasing <a href="/wiki/Reducing_agent" title="Reducing agent">reducing gases</a> at pressure, and so preventing the metal from penetrating the pores of the sand. It is also contained in 'mould wash', a paste or liquid with the same function applied to the mould before casting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> Sea coal can be mixed with the clay lining (the "bod") used for the bottom of a <a href="/wiki/Cupola_furnace" title="Cupola furnace">cupola furnace</a>. When heated, the coal decomposes and the bod becomes slightly friable, easing the process of breaking open holes for tapping the molten metal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Production_of_chemicals">Production of chemicals</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Coal_to_chemicals_routes_diagram.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="478" data-file-width="800" height="131" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Coal_to_chemicals_routes_diagram.jpg/220px-Coal_to_chemicals_routes_diagram.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Coal_to_chemicals_routes_diagram.jpg/330px-Coal_to_chemicals_routes_diagram.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Coal_to_chemicals_routes_diagram.jpg/440px-Coal_to_chemicals_routes_diagram.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Coal_to_chemicals_routes_diagram.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Production of chemicals from coal</div></div></div>
<p>Coal is an important feedstock in production of a wide range of chemical fertilizers and other chemical products.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> The main route to these products is <a href="/wiki/Coal_gasification" title="Coal gasification">coal gasification</a> to produce <a href="/wiki/Syngas" title="Syngas">syngas</a>. Primary chemicals that are produced directly from the syngas include <a href="/wiki/Methanol" title="Methanol">methanol</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a>, which are the chemical building blocks from which a whole spectrum of derivative chemicals are manufactured, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Olefins" title="Olefins">olefins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Acetic_acid" title="Acetic acid">acetic acid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Formaldehyde" title="Formaldehyde">formaldehyde</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Urea" title="Urea">urea</a> and others. The versatility of <a href="/wiki/Syngas" title="Syngas">syngas</a> as a precursor to primary chemicals and high-value derivative products provides the option of using relatively inexpensive coal to produce a wide range of valuable commodities.
</p><p>Historically, production of chemicals from coal has been used since the 1950s and has become established in the market. According to the 2010 Worldwide Gasification Database,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DOEGasificationDatabase2010_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-DOEGasificationDatabase2010-76">[76]</a></sup> a survey of current and planned gasifiers, from 2004 to 2007 chemical production increased its gasification product share from 37% to 45%. From 2008 to 2010, 22% of new gasifier additions were to be for chemical production.
</p><p>Because the slate of chemical products that can be made via coal gasification can in general also use feedstocks derived from <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">petroleum</a>, the chemical industry tends to use whatever feedstocks are most cost-effective. Therefore, interest in using coal tends to increase for higher oil and natural gas prices and during periods of high global economic growth that may strain oil and gas production. Also, production of chemicals from coal is of much higher interest in countries like South Africa, China, India and the United States where there are abundant coal resources. The abundance of coal combined with lack of natural gas resources in China is strong inducement for the coal to chemicals industry pursued there. In the United States, the best example of the industry is Eastman Chemical Company which has been successfully operating a coal-to-chemicals plant at its Kingsport, Tennessee, site since 1983. Similarly, Sasol has built and operated coal-to-chemicals facilities in South Africa.
</p><p>Coal to chemical processes do require substantial quantities of water. As of 2013 much of the coal to chemical production was in the People's Republic of China<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OILDRUM9371_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-OILDRUM9371-77">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ICIS022712_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-ICIS022712-78">[78]</a></sup> where environmental regulation and <a href="/wiki/Water_resource_management" title="Water resource management">water management</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IHT02513_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-IHT02513-79">[79]</a></sup> was weak.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT020213_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT020213-80">[80]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Coal_industry">Coal industry</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main pages: <a href="/wiki/Category:Coal_companies" title="Category:Coal companies">Category:Coal companies</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coal_mining" title="Coal mining">Coal mining</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coal_industry_in_China" title="Coal industry in China">Coal industry in China</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coal_industry_in_Pakistan" title="Coal industry in Pakistan">Coal industry in Pakistan</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coal_industry_in_India" title="Coal industry in India">Coal industry in India</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coal_industry_in_New_Zealand" title="Coal industry in New Zealand">Coal industry in New Zealand</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Coal_companies_of_Australia" title="Coal companies of Australia">Coal companies of Australia</a></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Coal_Industry_Nationalisation_Act_1946" title="Coal Industry Nationalisation Act 1946">Coal Industry Nationalisation Act 1946</a> and <a href="/wiki/Coal_Industry_Commission_Act_1919" title="Coal Industry Commission Act 1919">Coal Industry Commission Act 1919</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coal_as_a_traded_commodity">Coal as a traded commodity</span></h3>
<p>In North America, Central <a href="/wiki/Appalachia" title="Appalachia">Appalachian</a> coal <a href="/wiki/Futures_contract" title="Futures contract">futures contracts</a> are currently traded on the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Mercantile_Exchange" title="New York Mercantile Exchange">New York Mercantile Exchange</a> (trading symbol <i>QL</i>). The trading unit is 1,550 short tons (1,410 t) per contract, and is quoted in U.S. dollars and cents per ton. Since coal is the principal fuel for generating electricity in the United States, coal futures contracts provide coal producers and the <a href="/wiki/Electric_power_industry" title="Electric power industry">electric power industry</a> an important tool for <a href="/wiki/Hedge_(finance)" title="Hedge (finance)">hedging</a> and <a href="/wiki/Risk_management" title="Risk management">risk management</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup>
</p><p>In addition to the NYMEX contract, the <a href="/wiki/Intercontinental_Exchange" title="Intercontinental Exchange">Intercontinental Exchange</a> (ICE) has European (Rotterdam) and South African (Richards Bay) coal futures available for trading. The trading unit for these contracts is 5,000 tonnes (5,500 short tons), and are also quoted in U.S. dollars and cents per ton.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup>
</p><p>The price of coal increased from around $30.00 per <a href="/wiki/Short_ton" title="Short ton">short ton</a> in 2000 to around $150.00 per short ton as of September 2008. As of October 2008, the price per short ton had declined to $111.50. Prices further declined to $71.25 as of October 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> In early 2015, it was trading near $56/ton.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_and_health_effects">Environmental and health effects</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Environmental_impact_of_the_coal_industry" title="Environmental impact of the coal industry">Environmental impact of the coal industry</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Aerial_view_of_ash_slide_site_Dec_23_2008_TVA.gov_123002.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1024" data-file-width="828" height="272" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Aerial_view_of_ash_slide_site_Dec_23_2008_TVA.gov_123002.jpg/220px-Aerial_view_of_ash_slide_site_Dec_23_2008_TVA.gov_123002.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Aerial_view_of_ash_slide_site_Dec_23_2008_TVA.gov_123002.jpg/330px-Aerial_view_of_ash_slide_site_Dec_23_2008_TVA.gov_123002.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Aerial_view_of_ash_slide_site_Dec_23_2008_TVA.gov_123002.jpg/440px-Aerial_view_of_ash_slide_site_Dec_23_2008_TVA.gov_123002.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Aerial_view_of_ash_slide_site_Dec_23_2008_TVA.gov_123002.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Aerial photograph of the site of the <a href="/wiki/Kingston_Fossil_Plant" title="Kingston Fossil Plant">Kingston Fossil Plant</a> coal fly ash slurry spill taken the day after <a href="/wiki/Kingston_Fossil_Plant_coal_fly_ash_slurry_spill" title="Kingston Fossil Plant coal fly ash slurry spill">the event</a></div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Health_effects">Health effects</span></h3>
<p>The use of coal as fuel causes adverse health impacts and deaths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup>
</p><p>The deadly <a href="/wiki/Pea_soup_fog#London" title="Pea soup fog">London smog</a> was caused primarily by the heavy use of coal. In the United States coal-fired power plants were estimated in 2004 to cause nearly 24,000 premature deaths every year, including 2,800 from <a href="/wiki/Lung_cancer" title="Lung cancer">lung cancer</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> Annual health costs in Europe from use of coal to generate electricity are €42.8 billion, or $55 billion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> Yet the disease and mortality burden of coal use today falls most heavily upon China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup>
</p><p>Breathing in <a href="/wiki/Coal_dust" title="Coal dust">coal dust</a> causes <a href="/wiki/Coalworker%27s_pneumoconiosis" title="Coalworker's pneumoconiosis">coalworker's pneumoconiosis</a> which is known colloquially as "black lung", so-called because the coal dust literally turns the lungs black from their usual pink color.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> In the United States alone, it is estimated that 1,500 former employees of the coal industry die every year from the effects of breathing in coal mine dust.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup>
</p><p>Around 10% of coal is ash,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coal_ash" title="Coal ash">Coal ash</a> is hazardous and toxic to human beings and other living things.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> Coal ash contains the radioactive elements <a href="/wiki/Uranium" title="Uranium">uranium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thorium" title="Thorium">thorium</a>. Coal ash and other solid combustion byproducts are stored locally and escape in various ways that expose those living near coal plants to radiation and environmental toxics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sa-coal-ash-more-rdactiv_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-sa-coal-ash-more-rdactiv-95">[95]</a></sup>
</p><p>Huge amounts of coal ash and other waste is produced annually. In 2013, the US alone consumed on the order of 983 million short tonnes of coal per year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> Use of coal on this scale generates hundreds of millions of tons of ash and other waste products every year. These include <a href="/wiki/Fly_ash" title="Fly ash">fly ash</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bottom_ash" title="Bottom ash">bottom ash</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Flue-gas_desulfurization" title="Flue-gas desulfurization">flue-gas desulfurization</a> sludge, that contain <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(element)" title="Mercury (element)">mercury</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uranium" title="Uranium">uranium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thorium" title="Thorium">thorium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arsenic" title="Arsenic">arsenic</a>, and other <a href="/wiki/Heavy_metals" title="Heavy metals">heavy metals</a>, along with non-metals such as <a href="/wiki/Selenium" title="Selenium">selenium</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/American_Lung_Association" title="American Lung Association">American Lung Association</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/American_Nurses%27_Association" title="American Nurses' Association">American Nurses' Association</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Physicians_for_Social_Responsibility" title="Physicians for Social Responsibility">Physicians for Social Responsibility</a> released a report in 2009 which details in depth the detrimental impact of the coal industry on human health, including workers in the mines and individuals living in communities near plants burning coal as a power source. This report provides medical information regarding damage to the lungs, heart, and nervous system of Americans caused by the burning of coal as fuel. It details how the air pollution caused by the plume of coal smokestack emissions is a cause of asthma, strokes, reduced intelligence, artery blockages, heart attacks, congestive heart failure, cardiac arrhythmias, mercury poisoning, arterial occlusion, and lung cancer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-psr_coal-damages-health_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-psr_coal-damages-health-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>More recently, the Chicago School of Public Health released a largely similar report, echoing many of the same findings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup>
</p><p>Though coal burning has increasingly been supplanted by less-toxic natural gas use in recent years, a 2010 study by the Clean Air Task Force still estimated that "air pollution from coal-fired power plants accounts for more than 13,000 premature deaths, 20,000 heart attacks, and 1.6 million lost workdays in the U.S. each year." The total monetary cost of these health impacts is over $100 billion annually.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup>
</p><p>A 2017 study in the <i>Economic Journal</i> found that for Britain during the period 1851–1860, "a one standard deviation increase in coal use raised infant mortality by 6–8% and that industrial coal use explains roughly one-third of the urban mortality penalty observed during this period."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_effects">Environmental effects</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Coal_mining" title="Coal mining">Coal mining</a> and coal fueling of <a href="/wiki/Power_station" title="Power station">power station</a> and industrial processes can cause major environmental damage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup>
</p><p>Water systems are affected by coal mining.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> For example, mining affects <a href="/wiki/Groundwater" title="Groundwater">groundwater</a> and <a href="/wiki/Water_table" title="Water table">water table</a> levels and acidity. Spills of fly ash, such as the <a href="/wiki/Kingston_Fossil_Plant_coal_fly_ash_slurry_spill" title="Kingston Fossil Plant coal fly ash slurry spill">Kingston Fossil Plant coal fly ash slurry spill</a>, can also contaminate land and waterways, and destroy homes. Power stations that burn coal also consume large quantities of water. This can affect the flows of rivers, and has consequential impacts on other land uses.
</p><p>One of the earliest known impacts of coal on the <a href="/wiki/Water_cycle" title="Water cycle">water cycle</a> was <a href="/wiki/Acid_rain" title="Acid rain">acid rain</a>. Approximately 75 Tg/S per year of <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_dioxide" title="Sulfur dioxide">sulfur dioxide</a> (SO<sub>2</sub>) is released from burning coal. After release, the sulfur dioxide is oxidized to gaseous H<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>2</sub> which scatters solar radiation, hence its increase in the atmosphere exerts a cooling effect on climate. This beneficially masks some of the warming caused by increased greenhouse gases. However, the sulfur is precipitated out of the atmosphere as acid rain in a matter of weeks,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> whereas carbon dioxide remains in the atmosphere for hundreds of years. Release of SO<sub>2</sub> also contributes to the widespread acidification of ecosystems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup>
</p><p>Disused coal mines can also cause issues. Subsidence can occur above tunnels, causing damage to infrastructure or cropland. Coal mining can also cause long lasting fires, and it has been estimated that thousands of <a href="/wiki/Coal_seam_fire" title="Coal seam fire">coal seam fires</a> are burning at any given time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Deep_underground_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-Deep_underground-105">[105]</a></sup> For example, there is a <a href="/wiki/Coal_seam_fire#Germany" title="Coal seam fire">coal seam fire in Germany</a> that has been burning since 1668, and is still burning in the 21st century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mineralienatlas_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-mineralienatlas-106">[106]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some environmental impacts are modest, such as dust nuisance. However, perhaps the largest and most long term effect of coal use is the release of carbon dioxide, a <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gas</a> that causes <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a> and <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a>, according to the <a href="/wiki/Intergovernmental_Panel_on_Climate_Change" title="Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change">IPCC</a> and the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency" title="United States Environmental Protection Agency">EPA</a>. Coal is the largest contributor to the human-made increase of CO<sub>2</sub> in the atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup>
</p><p>The production of coke from coal produces <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a>, coal tar, and gaseous compounds as by-products which if discharged to land, air or waterways can act as environmental pollutants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Whyalla_steelworks" title="Whyalla steelworks">Whyalla steelworks</a> is one example of a coke producing facility where liquid ammonia is discharged to the marine environment.
</p><p>In 1999, world gross <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_emissions" title="Carbon dioxide emissions">carbon dioxide emissions</a> from coal usage were 8,666 million tonnes of carbon dioxide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> In 2011, world gross emissions from coal usage were 14,416 million tonnes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-US.EIA_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-US.EIA-110">[110]</a></sup> For every megawatt-hour generated, coal-fired electric power generation emits around 2,000 pounds of carbon dioxide, which is almost double the approximately 1100 pounds of carbon dioxide released by a <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a>-fired electric plant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> Because of this higher carbon efficiency of natural gas generation, as the market in the United States has changed to reduce coal and increase natural gas generation, carbon dioxide emissions may have fallen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> Those measured in the first quarter of 2012 were the lowest of any recorded for the first quarter of any year since 1992.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> In 2013, the head of the UN climate agency advised that most of the world's coal reserves should be left in the ground to avoid catastrophic global warming.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Clean_coal_technology">Clean coal technology</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Clean_coal_technology" title="Clean coal technology">Clean coal technology</a></div>
