<p>"Clean" coal technology is a collection of technologies being developed to mitigate the environmental impact of coal energy generation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> Those technologies are being developed to remove or reduce pollutant emissions to the atmosphere. Some of the techniques that would be used to accomplish this include chemically washing <a href="/wiki/Mineral" title="Mineral">minerals</a> and impurities from the coal, <a href="/wiki/Gasification" title="Gasification">gasification</a> (see also <a href="/wiki/Integrated_gasification_combined_cycle" title="Integrated gasification combined cycle">IGCC</a>), improved technology for treating <a href="/wiki/Flue" title="Flue">flue</a> gases to remove pollutants to increasingly stringent levels and at higher efficiency, <a href="/wiki/Carbon_capture_and_storage" title="Carbon capture and storage">carbon capture and storage</a> technologies to capture the carbon dioxide from the <a href="/wiki/Flue_gas" title="Flue gas">flue gas</a> and dewatering lower rank coals (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brown_coal" title="Brown coal">brown coals</a>) to improve the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Calorific_value" title="Calorific value">calorific value</a>, and thus the efficiency of the conversion into <a href="/wiki/Electricity" title="Electricity">electricity</a>. Figures from the United States Environmental Protection Agency show that these technologies have made today's coal-based generating fleet 77 percent cleaner on the basis of regulated emissions per unit of energy produced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup>
</p><p>Clean coal technology usually addresses atmospheric problems resulting from burning coal. Historically, the primary focus was on SO<sub>2</sub> and NO<sub>x</sub>, the most important gases in causation of <a href="/wiki/Acid_rain" title="Acid rain">acid rain</a>, and particulates which cause visible air pollution and deleterious effects on human health. More recent focus has been on carbon dioxide (due to its impact on <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> and concern over toxic species such as mercury.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> Concerns exist regarding the economic viability of these technologies and the timeframe of delivery,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> potentially high hidden economic costs in terms of social and environmental damage,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> and the costs and viability of disposing of removed carbon and other toxic matter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Oxyfuel_CCS_fossil_fuel_power_plant_operation.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="302" data-file-width="466" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Oxyfuel_CCS_fossil_fuel_power_plant_operation.png/220px-Oxyfuel_CCS_fossil_fuel_power_plant_operation.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Oxyfuel_CCS_fossil_fuel_power_plant_operation.png/330px-Oxyfuel_CCS_fossil_fuel_power_plant_operation.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Oxyfuel_CCS_fossil_fuel_power_plant_operation.png/440px-Oxyfuel_CCS_fossil_fuel_power_plant_operation.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Oxyfuel_CCS_fossil_fuel_power_plant_operation.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An oxyfuel CCS power plant operation processes the exhaust gases so as to separate the CO<sub>2</sub> so that it may be stored or sequestered</div></div></div>
<p>Several different technological methods are available for the purpose of carbon capture as demanded by the clean coal concept:
</p>
<ul><li>Pre-combustion capture – This involves <a href="/wiki/Gasification" title="Gasification">gasification</a> of a feedstock (such as coal) to form synthesis gas, which may be <a href="/wiki/Water-gas_shift_reaction" title="Water-gas shift reaction">shifted</a> to produce a H<sub>2</sub> and CO<sub>2</sub>-rich gas mixture, from which the CO<sub>2</sub> can be efficiently captured and separated, transported, and ultimately sequestered,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> This technology is usually associated with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Integrated_Gasification_Combined_Cycle" title="Integrated Gasification Combined Cycle">Integrated Gasification Combined Cycle</a> process configurations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup></li>
<li>Post-combustion capture – This refers to capture of CO<sub>2</sub> from exhaust gases of combustion processes, typically using sorbents, solvents, or membrane separations to remove CO<sub>2</sub> from the bulk gases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxy-fuel_combustion" title="Oxy-fuel combustion">Oxy-fuel combustion</a> – Fossil fuels such as coal are burned in a mixture of recirculated flue gas and oxygen, rather than in air, which largely eliminates nitrogen from the flue gas enabling efficient, low-cost CO<sub>2</sub> capture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Kemper_Project" title="Kemper Project">Kemper County IGCC Project</a>, a 582 MW <a href="/wiki/Coal_gasification" title="Coal gasification">coal gasification</a>-based power plant, will use pre-combustion capture of CO<sub>2</sub> to capture 65% of the CO<sub>2</sub> the plant produces, which will be utilized/geologically sequestered in <a href="/wiki/Enhanced_oil_recovery" title="Enhanced oil recovery">enhanced oil recovery</a> operations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> If the technology used at the Kemper Project is successful, it will be the United States’ first clean coal plant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Saskatchewan Government's <a href="/wiki/Boundary_Dam_Power_Station" title="Boundary Dam Power Station">Boundary Dam Power Station</a> Integrated Carbon Capture and Sequestration Demonstration Project will use post-combustion, amine-based scrubber technology to capture 90% of the CO<sub>2</sub> emitted by Unit 3 of the power plant; this CO<sub>2</sub> will be pipelined to and utilized for enhanced oil recovery in the Weyburn oil fields.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> However, only about a half of this CO2 will actually be permanently stored, the remainder is released into the atmosphere during capturing, and the processing in the oil field.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup>
</p><p>An early example of a coal-based plant using (oxy-fuel) carbon-capture technology is Swedish company <a href="/wiki/Vattenfall" title="Vattenfall">Vattenfall</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Schwarze_Pumpe_power_station" title="Schwarze Pumpe power station">Schwarze Pumpe power station</a> located in <a href="/wiki/Spremberg" title="Spremberg">Spremberg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a>, built by German firm <a href="/wiki/Siemens" title="Siemens">Siemens</a>, which went on-line in September 2008.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-discovermagazine_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-discovermagazine-132">[132]</a></sup> The facility captures CO<sub>2</sub> and acid rain producing pollutants, separates them, and compresses the CO<sub>2</sub> into a liquid. Plans are to inject the CO<sub>2</sub> into depleted natural gas fields or other geological formations. Vattenfall opines that this technology is considered not to be a final solution for CO<sub>2</sub> reduction in the atmosphere, but provides an achievable solution in the near term while more desirable alternative solutions to power generation can be made economically practical.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-discovermagazine_132-1"><a href="#cite_note-discovermagazine-132">[132]</a></sup> In 2014  research and development were discontinued due to high costs making the technology unviable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thelocal.se_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-thelocal.se-133">[133]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Bioremediation">Bioremediation</span></h2>
<p>The white rot fungus <i><a href="/wiki/Trametes_versicolor" title="Trametes versicolor">Trametes versicolor</a></i> can grow on and metabolize naturally occurring coal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> The bacteria <a href="/wiki/Diplococcus" title="Diplococcus">Diplococcus</a> has been found to degrade coal, raising its temperature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economic_aspects">Economic aspects</span></h2>
<p>Coal (by liquefaction technology) is one of the <a href="/wiki/Backstop_resources" title="Backstop resources">backstop resources</a> that could limit escalation of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oil_prices" title="Oil prices">oil prices</a> and mitigate the effects of transportation energy shortage that will occur under <a href="/wiki/Peak_oil" title="Peak oil">peak oil</a>. This is contingent on liquefaction production capacity becoming large enough to satiate the very large and growing demand for petroleum. Estimates of the cost of producing liquid fuels from coal suggest that domestic U.S. production of fuel from coal becomes cost-competitive with oil priced at around $35 per barrel,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup> with the $35 being the break-even cost. With oil prices as low as around $40 per barrel in the U.S. as of December 2008, liquid coal lost some of its economic allure in the U.S., but will probably be re-vitalized, similar to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oil_sand" title="Oil sand">oil sand</a> projects, with an oil price around $70 per barrel.
</p><p>In China, due to an increasing need for liquid energy in the transportation sector, coal liquefaction projects were given high priority even during periods of oil prices below $40 per barrel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> This is probably because China prefers not to be dependent on foreign oil, instead utilizing its enormous domestic coal reserves. As oil prices were increasing during the first half of 2009, the coal liquefaction projects in China were again boosted, and these projects are profitable with an oil barrel price of $40.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup>
</p><p>China is the largest producer of coal in the world. It is the world's largest energy consumer, and relies on coal to supply 69% of its energy needs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> An estimated 5 million people worked in China's coal-mining industry in 2007.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup>
</p><p>Coal pollution costs the EU €43 billion each year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup> Measures to cut air pollution may have beneficial long-term economic impacts for individuals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Energy_density_and_carbon_impact">Energy density and carbon impact</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Energy_value_of_coal" title="Energy value of coal">Energy value of coal</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Energy_density" title="Energy density">energy density</a> of coal, i.e. its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heating_value" title="Heating value">heating value</a>, is roughly 24 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Megajoule" title="Megajoule">megajoules</a> per kilogram<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup> (approximately 6.7 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kilowatt-hours" title="Kilowatt-hours">kilowatt-hours</a> per kg). For a coal power plant with a 40% efficiency, it takes an estimated 325 kg (717 lb) of coal to power a 100 W lightbulb for one year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup>
</p><p>As of 2006, the average efficiency of electricity-generating power stations was 31%; in 2002, coal represented about 23% of total global energy supply, an equivalent of 3.4 billion tonnes of coal, of which 2.8 billion tonnes were used for electricity generation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup>
</p><p>The US Energy Information Agency's 1999 report on CO<sub>2</sub> emissions for energy generation quotes an emission factor of 0.963 kg CO<sub>2</sub>/kWh for coal power, compared to 0.881 kg CO<sub>2</sub>/kWh (oil), or 0.569 kg CO<sub>2</sub>/kWh (natural gas).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Underground_fires">Underground fires</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Coal_seam_fire" title="Coal seam fire">Coal seam fire</a></div>
<p>Thousands of coal fires are burning around the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> Those burning underground can be difficult to locate and many cannot be extinguished. Fires can cause the ground above to subside, their combustion gases are dangerous to life, and breaking out to the surface can initiate surface <a href="/wiki/Wildfire" title="Wildfire">wildfires</a>. Coal seams can be set on fire by <a href="/wiki/Spontaneous_combustion" title="Spontaneous combustion">spontaneous combustion</a> or contact with a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mine_fire" title="Mine fire">mine fire</a> or surface fire. Lightning strikes are an important source of ignition. The coal continues to burn slowly back into the seam until oxygen (air) can no longer reach the flame front. A grass fire in a coal area can set dozens of coal seams on fire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup> Coal fires in China burn an estimated 120 million tons of coal a year, emitting 360 million metric tons of CO<sub>2</sub>, amounting to 2–3% of the annual worldwide production of CO<sub>2</sub> from <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">fossil fuels</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Centralia,_Pennsylvania" title="Centralia, Pennsylvania">Centralia, Pennsylvania</a> (a <a href="/wiki/Borough_(Pennsylvania)" title="Borough (Pennsylvania)">borough</a> located in the <a href="/wiki/Coal_Region" title="Coal Region">Coal Region</a> of the United States), an exposed vein of anthracite ignited in 1962 due to a trash fire in the borough landfill, located in an abandoned <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anthracite_coal" title="Anthracite coal">anthracite</a> <a href="/wiki/Surface_mining" title="Surface mining">strip mine</a> pit. Attempts to extinguish the fire were unsuccessful, and it <a href="/wiki/Centralia_mine_fire" title="Centralia mine fire">continues to burn underground to this day</a>. The Australian <a href="/wiki/Burning_Mountain" title="Burning Mountain">Burning Mountain</a> was originally believed to be a volcano, but the smoke and ash come from a coal fire that has been burning for some 6,000 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup>
</p><p>At <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Kuh_i_Malik&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Kuh i Malik (page does not exist)">Kuh i Malik</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yagnob_Valley" title="Yagnob Valley">Yagnob Valley</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tajikistan" title="Tajikistan">Tajikistan</a>, coal deposits have been burning for thousands of years, creating vast underground labyrinths full of unique minerals, some of them very beautiful. Local people once used this method<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (December 2014)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> to mine <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ammoniac" title="Ammoniac">ammoniac</a>. This place has been well-known since the time of <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a>, but European geographers misinterpreted the Ancient Greek descriptions as the evidence of active <a href="/wiki/Volcanism" title="Volcanism">volcanism</a> in <a href="/wiki/Turkestan" title="Turkestan">Turkestan</a> (up to the 19th century, when the Russian army invaded the area).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>The reddish siltstone rock that caps many ridges and buttes in the <a href="/wiki/Powder_River_Basin" title="Powder River Basin">Powder River Basin</a> in <a href="/wiki/Wyoming" title="Wyoming">Wyoming</a> and in western <a href="/wiki/North_Dakota" title="North Dakota">North Dakota</a> is called <i>porcelanite</i>, which resembles the coal burning waste "clinker" or volcanic "<a href="/wiki/Scoria" title="Scoria">scoria</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup> Clinker is rock that has been fused by the natural burning of coal. In the Powder River Basin approximately 27 to 54 billion tons of coal burned within the past three million years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup> Wild coal fires in the area were reported by the <a href="/wiki/Lewis_and_Clark_Expedition" title="Lewis and Clark Expedition">Lewis and Clark Expedition</a> as well as explorers and settlers in the area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Market_trends">Market trends</span></h2>
<p>In 2006, China was the top producer of coal with 38% share followed by the United States and India, according to the <a href="/wiki/British_Geological_Survey" title="British Geological Survey">British Geological Survey</a>. As of 2012 coal production in the United States was falling at the rate of 7% annually<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EIA120312_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-EIA120312-156">[156]</a></sup> with many power plants using coal shut down or converted to natural gas; however, some of the reduced domestic demand was taken up by increased exports<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT121912_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT121912-157">[157]</a></sup> with five <a href="/wiki/Coal_pier" title="Coal pier">coal export terminals</a> being proposed in the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Northwest" title="Pacific Northwest">Pacific Northwest</a> to export coal from the <a href="/wiki/Powder_River_Basin" title="Powder River Basin">Powder River Basin</a> to China and other Asian markets;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SFC032513_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-SFC032513-158">[158]</a></sup> however, as of 2013, environmental opposition was increasing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT61413_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT61413-159">[159]</a></sup> High-sulfur coal mined in Illinois which was unsaleable in the United States found a ready market in Asia as exports reached 13 million tons in 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SFC5113_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-SFC5113-160">[160]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="World_coal_reserves">World coal reserves</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Coal_mine_Wyoming.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="600" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Coal_mine_Wyoming.jpg/220px-Coal_mine_Wyoming.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Coal_mine_Wyoming.jpg/330px-Coal_mine_Wyoming.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Coal_mine_Wyoming.jpg/440px-Coal_mine_Wyoming.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Coal_mine_Wyoming.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A coal mine in <a href="/wiki/Wyoming" title="Wyoming">Wyoming</a>, United States. The United States has the world's largest coal reserves.</div></div></div>
<p>The 948 billion short tons of recoverable coal reserves estimated by the Energy Information Administration are equal to about 4,196 BBOE (billion <a href="/wiki/Barrel_of_oil_equivalent" title="Barrel of oil equivalent">barrels of oil equivalent</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EIAreserves_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-EIAreserves-38">[38]</a></sup> The amount of coal burned during 2007 was estimated at 7.075 billion short tons, or 133.179 quadrillion BTUs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup> This is an average of 18.8 million BTU per short ton. In terms of heat content, this is about 57,000,000 barrels (9,100,000 m<sup>3</sup>) of oil equivalent per day. By comparison in 2007, natural gas provided 51,000,000 barrels (8,100,000 m<sup>3</sup>) of oil equivalent per day, while oil provided 85,800,000 barrels (13,640,000 m<sup>3</sup>) per day.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/BP" title="BP">British Petroleum</a>, in its 2007 report, estimated at 2006 end that there were 147 years <a href="/wiki/Reserves-to-production_ratio" title="Reserves-to-production ratio">reserves-to-production ratio</a> based on <i>proven</i> coal reserves worldwide. This figure only includes reserves classified as "proven"; exploration drilling programs by mining companies, particularly in under-explored areas, are continually providing new reserves. In many cases, companies are aware of coal deposits that have not been sufficiently drilled to qualify as "proven". However, some nations haven't updated their information and assume reserves remain at the same levels even with withdrawals.
</p><p>Of the three fossil fuels, coal has the most widely distributed reserves; coal is mined in over 100 countries, and on all continents except Antarctica. The largest reserves are found in the United States, Russia, China, Australia and India. Note the table below.

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Major_coal_producers">Major coal producers</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_coal_production" title="List of countries by coal production">List of countries by coal production</a></div>
<p>The reserve life is an estimate based only on current production levels and proved reserves level for the countries shown, and makes no assumptions of future production or even current production trends. Countries with annual production higher than 100 million tonnes are shown. For comparison, data for the European Union is also shown. Shares are based on data expressed in tonnes oil equivalent.
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Major_coal_exporters">Major coal exporters</span></h3>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Major_coal_importers">Major coal importers</span></h3>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_usage">Cultural usage</span></h2>
<p>Coal is the <a href="/wiki/List_of_U.S._state_minerals,_rocks,_stones_and_gemstones" title="List of U.S. state minerals, rocks, stones and gemstones">official state mineral</a> of <a href="/wiki/Kentucky" title="Kentucky">Kentucky</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kentucky_mineral_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kentucky_mineral-169">[169]</a></sup> and the official state rock of <a href="/wiki/Utah" title="Utah">Utah</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Utah_rock_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-Utah_rock-170">[170]</a></sup> both <a href="/wiki/U.S._state" title="U.S. state">U.S. states</a> have a historic link to coal mining.
</p><p>Some cultures hold that children who misbehave will receive only a lump of coal from <a href="/wiki/Santa_Claus" title="Santa Claus">Santa Claus</a> for Christmas in their <a href="/wiki/Christmas_stocking" title="Christmas stocking">christmas stockings</a> instead of presents.
</p><p>It is also customary and considered lucky in Scotland and the North of England to give coal as a gift on New Year's Day. This occurs as part of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First-Foot" title="First-Foot">First-Footing</a> and represents warmth for the year to come.
</p>
